    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15653853407810946248">This controversial bestselling novel in the Arab world reveals the political corruption, sexual repression, religious extremism, and modern hopes of Egypt today.<br/><br/>All manner of flawed and fragile humanity reside in the Yacoubian Building, a once-elegant temple of Art Deco splendor now slowly decaying in the smog and bustle of downtown Cairo: a fading aristocrat and self-proc</span>
  <span id="freeText15653853407810946248" style="display:none">This controversial bestselling novel in the Arab world reveals the political corruption, sexual repression, religious extremism, and modern hopes of Egypt today.<br /><br />All manner of flawed and fragile humanity reside in the Yacoubian Building, a once-elegant temple of Art Deco splendor now slowly decaying in the smog and bustle of downtown Cairo: a fading aristocrat and self-proclaimed "scientist of women"; a sultry, voluptuous siren; a devout young student, feeling the irresistible pull toward fundamentalism; a newspaper editor helplessly in love with a policeman; a corrupt and corpulent politician, twisting the Koran to justify his desires.<br /><br />These disparate lives careen toward an explosive conclusion in Alaa Al Aswany's remarkable international bestseller. Teeming with frank sexuality and heartfelt compassion, this book is an important window on to the experience of loss and love in the Arab world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15653853407810946248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/251441-which-of-the-many-interesting-characters">Which of the many interesting characters did you like best in Alaa Al-Aswany&#39;s debut novel that first took Egypt by storm and then quickly became an international bestseller?</a>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/1183221-susan"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1211593859i/1183221.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/1183221-susan">Susan</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">My favorite was Busayna because I could very slightly identify with some of her predicament in the workplace (from long, long ago). She had practicali<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">My favorite was Busayna because I could very slightly identify with some of her predicament in the workplace (from long, long ago). She had practicality and perseverance despite her setbacks. She accepted her responsibility to take care of her family despite the small ability she had as a woman to provide for them. She was annoyed by Taha's unwillingness to accept life as it was, and she recognized love when it came, no matter how strange a place it came from. I had sympathy for Taha in the beginning but his inability to accept and adapt is an annoying quality found in many men who put pride before life, where women most often shoulder the burden and keep on going. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_176524519" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176524519" />
    <a title="·Karen·" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652898-karen"><img alt="·Karen·" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176524519?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="·Karen·" class="user" itemprop="url" name="·Karen·" href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783857877230" href="/book/show/6386612-der-jakubij-n-bau">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=mttbr-2012">mttbr-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1652898-karen?shelf=in-german">in-german</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176524519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6356495783087256512"><b>Dispiriting. </b><br/><br/>I nearly tossed this into the nearest patch of long grass, as I got so thoroughly sick of the descriptions of boobs and buttocks, so impatient with the fact that ALL the women fell into only two categories: young and therefore luscious and desirable, or old, and therefore no longer desirable. Worst of all is old and skinny - and yes, it says a LOT about me that I'm particularly sensitive to this - because old and skinny means you turn into a screeching termagant. (Somebody better wa</span>
  <span id="freeText6356495783087256512" style="display:none"><b>Dispiriting. </b><br /><br />I nearly tossed this into the nearest patch of long grass, as I got so thoroughly sick of the descriptions of boobs and buttocks, so impatient with the fact that ALL the women fell into only two categories: young and therefore luscious and desirable, or old, and therefore no longer desirable. Worst of all is old and skinny - and yes, it says a LOT about me that I'm particularly sensitive to this - because old and skinny means you turn into a screeching termagant. (Somebody better warn my husband - what do you mean too late? HOW DARE YOU, I'LL HAVE YOUR GUTS FOR GARTERS.....) Old and chunky on the other hand means you're at least allowed to be motherly, sorry, grandmotherly. Even towards men who are older than you. So that puts <b>me</b> on a strict regime of chocolate, ice cream, cream cake and biscuits, and NO SPORT. <br /><br />There is an awful lot of sex in this book. Or should that be a lot of awful sex? Well, yes. <br /><i>Their intercourse on the first night was simple and spontaneous, as though she had been his wife for years. The rose opened to the touch of his fingers and he watered it more than once till it was quenched.</i><br /> Cringe. Maybe that sounds better in the original. <br />But it's not just the ho-hum description, it's that sex is a marketable commodity. That rose quotation describes 'simple and spontaneous' congruence between Taha and the wife chosen for him by the Islamist brotherhood. It is unique in the whole novel that it does <b>not</b> involve the obvious blandishments of money, or security, or the slightly more subtle pretence of a considerate sensual dexterity that fools the young (luscious, desirable) victim into believing that the old lecher actually cares for her on any other level than pleasuring her and proving his amazing ability to do so.<br /><br />But then, since I'm an old skinny harridan with a couple of brain cells to rub together, I began to think about WHY this made me so angry. And it was the story of Suad, who desperately and fruitlessly tries to take control of her own body, who hopes to gain some dignity and human value by producing a child (and is so shockingly frustrated in her aim) that pointed up to me that in a society where women have no economic or political influence, the only thing that's left to them is the power to bestow sexual favours. And the portrayal of homosexuality seems cliched in its sordidness, but then how can it be anything else in a society that makes something so natural illegal? <br /><br />So this is what al-Aswany does. He uses these rather one-dimensional figures to present what was wrong with Egyptian society under Mubarak. And it reads a lot like trashy soap opera, the rather simplistic causality between poverty, despotic rule and abuse on one side and religious radicalisation on the other. Aesthetically, not a delight. But a drastic situation requires drastic means. And if this is your view of the Egyptian people,<br /><br /><i>Our Lord created the Egyptians to accept government authority. No Egyptian can go against his government. Some peoples are excitable and rebellious by nature but the Egyptian keeps his head down his whole life long so he can eat. It says so in the history books. The Egyptians are the easiest people in the world to rule. The moment you take power, they submit to you and grovel to you and you can do what you want with them.</i><br /><br />(thankfully proved wrong by the events of the Arab Spring), then it's drastic means that you need. <br /><br />And you know what's really dispiriting? The nagging suspicion that Western readers will mistake a version of what's wrong with the system for a proof of what's wrong with the people. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6356495783087256512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26696649" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26696649" />
    <a title="Tyler " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1096417-tyler"><img alt="Tyler " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377822301p2/1096417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26696649?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyler " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyler " href="/user/show/1096417-tyler">Tyler </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789774248627" href="/book/show/978537.The_Yacoubian_Building">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those trying to make sense of the Middle East</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tyler  by: </span>
      _An Egyptian 
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1096417-tyler?shelf=gay-interest">gay-interest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26696649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer670130711697622450">Narrators tell stories; protagonists tell them; characters in novels do, too. But in <i>The Yacoubian Building</i>, an apartment complex on a downtown street tells the story of a whole nation. This ten-story structure, I found, has a lot to say.<br/><br/>The building doesn't talk, of course, but it shelters the many people whose lives the book recounts. Brought together only by their place of residence, these very different people are, by the end, brought together in a second way, by the common experience of lif</span>
  <span id="freeText670130711697622450" style="display:none">Narrators tell stories; protagonists tell them; characters in novels do, too. But in <i>The Yacoubian Building</i>, an apartment complex on a downtown street tells the story of a whole nation. This ten-story structure, I found, has a lot to say.<br /><br />The building doesn't talk, of course, but it shelters the many people whose lives the book recounts. Brought together only by their place of residence, these very different people are, by the end, brought together in a second way, by the common experience of life in modern Egypt. <br /><br />Just what it's like to live in Egypt is the real story. No number of articles about the Middle East can convey a feeling, so instead of telling us directly, the author paints a picture, a mosaic with a gestalt to it that makes the whole picture of Egypt suddenly pop into view. This shocking image is ... a sight.<br /><br />The character sketches at the start of the book give readers a glimpse of the daily lives and passions of the denizens of the building, who represent every class of society. As they go about their lives, the latter portion of the narrative shows them stumbling into a world bearing a sordid resemblance to something George Orwell might have concocted.<br /><br />In this world, it goes without saying that merit is seldom rewarded. Money, not law, makes things happen, even bad things. People wake up at night to find the police in their living rooms -- or maybe thugs hired by some rich man. Anyone can end up in a police station at any time, some never again to be seen. Torture, brutality, beatings and weird, weird sexism constitute everyday hazards.<br /><br />The story of the building itself, told mainly through the eyes of a long-time resident, stands as a proxy for the bittersweet change that has swept over Egypt in the past two generations. This book hints at the actual sources of Egypt's current problems; in reality, people dare not discuss them openly.<br /><br />Curiously, the book cannot be said to have an entirely negative feel. Egyptians simply don't have it in them to collapse into a Russian-style pessimism. In the book's moral I found its power: The narrative conveys not one, but many messages about the state of Egyptian society. In each you can find yourself saying, "So what has become obvious is ..." The book allows you to think about many things as you read.<br /><br />Readers will like the artful approach the author employs, as the choice of a building to tell the story implies. Al-Aswany relates his characters with a confidential tone, as if he were in a private conversation with us. His candid remarks about each character tell us everything we need to know about the character without saying too much. The story neither droops into sentimentality nor takes a desultory turn. The writing is clean and efficient, with the right things said at the right time using no extra words.<br /><br />The stories themselves are enough to stagger the senses, considering the society from which they emerge. The book has been a bestseller in Egypt, its subject matter so controversial that people never run out of things to say about it.<br /><br />I rate this a four -- I really like it. If it were a little longer, I'd give it a five because it's that good. I highly recommend <i>The Yacoubian Building</i> for anyone who likes good literature, and for Westerners in search of realistic insight into the question, "What on earth is going on over there?" <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="670130711697622450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_419020280" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Dawn Bates" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/445832-dawn-bates"><img alt="Dawn Bates" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556053715p2/445832.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/419020280?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dawn Bates" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dawn Bates" href="/user/show/445832-dawn-bates">Dawn Bates</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007243624" href="/book/show/1103556.The_Yacoubian_Building">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer419020280" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12344738526390790666">I've read this book twice now. Once before coming to Egypt and again this summer, having lived in Egypt for two years.<br/><br/>The first time I read it, I truly believed that the situations that happened within it were exaggerated for artistic license. Now? It's all true. No artistic license needed. Just straight forward truth. I see so many of these things happening, and many more besides.<br/><br/>Al Aswany's depiction of Egyptian life, his clever way of writing about Egypt's elite and corrupt, making sure reade</span>
  <span id="freeText12344738526390790666" style="display:none">I've read this book twice now. Once before coming to Egypt and again this summer, having lived in Egypt for two years.<br /><br />The first time I read it, I truly believed that the situations that happened within it were exaggerated for artistic license. Now? It's all true. No artistic license needed. Just straight forward truth. I see so many of these things happening, and many more besides.<br /><br />Al Aswany's depiction of Egyptian life, his clever way of writing about Egypt's elite and corrupt, making sure readers in the know know who he is on about, without actually naming names, is superb. <br /><br />As I read page after page, I recognised the some of the unnamed, and the named, areas, the hang outs, the streets, I couldn't believe how much more I enjoyed the book, and was deeply saddened by it. It also felt as though I was reading a completely different book. The image I had in my head after reading it the first time was that it was 'nus-nus' (50-50) of reality and fiction, now it's 'maya maya' 100% Egyptian life.<br /><br />I am surprised it was ever allowed to be published during the 'falool' (old regime) period of Egypt, or that Al Aswany's life wasn't the target of political imprisonment or the secret police... <br /><br />Some of the subjects are so taboo in Egyptian life that I am surprised the morality police were not all over it. It made me laugh, it made me angry, especially when the bawaab's son (caretaker's son) was sidelined from following his dream that led to his ultimate extremism... as is the case in so many cultures and countries, not just Egypt.<br /><br />A great read that is also a cultural lesson through fiction. Read it and learn about all the things Egyptians would rather you forget about, and learn about the things that led to the most impressive revolutions the world has seen to date.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12344738526390790666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_58069229" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58069229" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jenny (Reading Envy) by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1018356-rima">Rima</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-egypt">location-egypt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58069229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3171813399452124514">A handful of intertwining stories all passing through The Yacoubian Building in downtown Cairo, I read this in one plane trip from Texas to SC. I couldn't put it down! And not just for the sexual content, which I'm sure is the reason half the people who read this pick it up. Controversial in Egypt? Oh my! It has interesting insight into relationships of all kinds - homosexual men in a society that may look the other way if they like you enough, women who marry to support their families, and the </span>
  <span id="freeText3171813399452124514" style="display:none">A handful of intertwining stories all passing through The Yacoubian Building in downtown Cairo, I read this in one plane trip from Texas to SC. I couldn't put it down! And not just for the sexual content, which I'm sure is the reason half the people who read this pick it up. Controversial in Egypt? Oh my! It has interesting insight into relationships of all kinds - homosexual men in a society that may look the other way if they like you enough, women who marry to support their families, and the rights of children after the death of a parent.<br /><br />"At rare and exceptional moments Souad Gaber appears as she really is. A look suddenly flashes from her eyes like a spark and her face recovers its original appearance, exactly as an actor returns to his own character on finishing a role, takes off his costume, and wipes the makeup off his face. On such occasions, a serious, slowly awakening look suggestive of a certain degree of hardness and determination appears on Souad's face and reveals her true nature... that spark will flash in her eyes confirming that her mind never stops working, even in the heat of passion."<br /><br />In an interesting conversation about patriotism:<br />"A person has to love his country because his country is his mother. Does anyone hate his mother?"<br />(I'd say that's a pretty loaded question.)</span>

  <span id="freeText15791238228317971124" style="display:none">Egypt is in the news today for all the wrong reasons. But when I witness the turmoil there, I perceive a silver lining: this is the birth pain of a true democracy.<br /><br />I have had a lifelong love affair with Egypt, ever since I studied about pharaohs and the pyramids and hieroglyphics in middle school. I have seen the similarity with India, the paradox of being immensely rich culturally and dirt poor monetarily. Visiting the country had been my secret dream, which was realised three years ago.<br /><br />I read this novel before visiting Egypt: and after my visit, I could only marvel at how Aswany has succeeded in bringing the multifaceted country under one roof, that of the Yacoubian building. Capturing the macrocosm in the microcosm, when done by gifted writers, produces wonders.<br /><br />I am increasing my rating from 3 to 4 stars on second thoughts.<br /><br />I pray the events in Egypt, for all the tragedy and angst, end on a positive note - like the novel.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer272574339149444493">This book was a lot better than I expected and managed to narrate a lot of extremely difficult and taboo topics (in accordance with Egyptian/Islamic norms), without passing judgement or creating cartoonishly evil characters. Even the characters one finds abhorrent are genuinely sympathetic. It helped me understand views and perspectives completely alien to me and gave me a slightly better understanding of Egyptian society. Also, somewhat entertaining. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_65105250" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65105250" />
    <a title="okyrhoe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/825356-okyrhoe"><img alt="okyrhoe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373221413p2/825356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65105250?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="okyrhoe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="okyrhoe" href="/user/show/825356-okyrhoe">okyrhoe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007243624" href="/book/show/1103556.The_Yacoubian_Building">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/825356-okyrhoe?shelf=bookcrossing">bookcrossing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/825356-okyrhoe?shelf=egypt">egypt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer65105250&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65105250" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18066005822591518803">I enjoyed reading The Yacoubian Building but I can't resist analyzing its weaknesses.<br/><br/>The premise of The Yacoubian Building is a credible one. I did not sense that the setting of the story, the placement of a varied selection of individuals in close proximity with one another, all residents in one building is merely fictional license*. The novel is a frank & representative depiction of contemporary Egyptian urban settings, with enough historical and cultural background to explain the finer detail</span>
  <span id="freeText18066005822591518803" style="display:none">I enjoyed reading The Yacoubian Building but I can't resist analyzing its weaknesses.<br /><br />The premise of The Yacoubian Building is a credible one. I did not sense that the setting of the story, the placement of a varied selection of individuals in close proximity with one another, all residents in one building is merely fictional license*. The novel is a frank &amp; representative depiction of contemporary Egyptian urban settings, with enough historical and cultural background to explain the finer details of each character’s history and motivations.<br />Yet there is something missing in this tapestry. In this group portrait, the intertwining of the separate threads, although executed in vibrant and expressive strokes, seems to have been drawn at the expense of any depth of focus. There is no clear primary character, nor a central question that is developed to a sufficiently complex degree to make this a novel of substance where the reader can either empathize with a character or be intrigued by a theme that develops as the story progresses.<br />There’s a lack of originality in terms of authorial persepctive/intention, and the closing scenes of each character’s subplot don’t quite follow from what precedes them, so that ultimately the characters are reduced to moralistic/symbolic entities rather than multidimensional personalities. The characters begin their tales interestingly enough, but ultimately their respective closures appear forced, as if there is only one way their story could end, as if they are conforming to convention (even if the convention is a fictive one). <br />In the case of Busayna, with regard to her financial concerns, it would seem to me that she would be more likely to take a more practical route, rather than resort to the ‘honorable’ solution (marriage) to her existential dilemma. In terms of character development Busayna’s and Zaki’s pairing doesn’t follow through from their prior behavior or motivations. The union of old (age as well as tradition) with the new/young is schematic rather than realistic, a compromise rather than a progression. <br />Similarly, Abduh’s and Hatim’s climactic confrontation contextually does not add up, given the way their relationship was presented so far. Hatim’s outbursts seem far beneath his prior composure and generosity, esp. after all that he’s done for Abduh. The decline of Hatim’s behavior is a throwback to East Mediterranean cultural cliches of several decades ago – as with the overall nostalgia of the novel for an Egypt which no longer exists - when popular culture mandated that sexual transgression is a force that ultimately implodes upon itself. <br />Granted, Al Aswany pushes the limits, within the scope of Egyptian cultural realities, by presenting Abduh &amp; Hatim’s relationship in explicit terms, and by giving that relationship equal time and exposure in relation to the other parallel plots of the novel. Yet the conclusion is color-by-numbers what is expected within the reality of a society still in the process of evolving from feudal/patriarchal behavioral motivations to contemporary standards. I cannot fully comprehend El Aswany’s point, in terms of any possible socio-political commentary there might be in this anachronism. <br /><br />As a critique of contemporary Arab society where ‘men’ regardless of position and background, no longer operate according to the spirit of patriarchal codes of conduct I feel that the novel is incomplete. All sense of justice, faith, honor, respect seems to have decayed into crass abuse of the system which gives them power. The social confusion over these social values is most evident when the youthful Taha, whose male honor has been violated, seeks redress in an unmanly &amp; dishonorable way. But I venture that it's only the non-Arab reader who sees this, for according to the tribal traditions, both Abduh and Taha have cleansed themselves of dishonor by avenging their respective oppressors. On the other hand, for the women there is no apparent redress, as in the example of Busayna when her honor is violated (the public humiliation at being arrested for 'prostitution') - is her restitution only possible through marriage?<br /><br />It appears that Al Aswany has nothing new to say regarding the juxtaposition between the strict moral codes women must still (appear to) adhere to, an anachronistic tradition that persists despite a governing system proclaiming itself as modern, democratic, socialist, etc. We observe members of the political system and the religious administration as men who unashamedly foregoes ideological principles when it is convenient to their shortterm aims – the sheikh agreeing to convince Souad to have an abortion when pressured by his longtime financial benefactor, and when men enter politics only for the purpose of securing financial success through extortion and bribery, also when radical religious leaders force a young recruit to a marriage with a “martyr’s” widow, even as they are planning the young man’s own martyrdom, and condemning the bride to a second round of widowhood. Even she, the martyr’s widow that Taha is set up with in the Brotherhood training camp, is regressing into patriarchal/feudal traditions since she believes progress &amp; change is possible only by a return of the old codes of honor. <br /><br />Does Al Aswany conclude that the situation cannot be rectified when the men resisting it are themselves oppressors of men as well as women? I’m not sure that it can be interpreted with conviction. In this respect, I felt the novel is incomplete, that whatever lasting message the novel intended to convey has been left unsaid. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18066005822591518803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1072241668" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1072241668" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1072241668?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1072241668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12577118735065959178">A soap opera story of the people who live in a Cairo mid-rise apartment building. We have all walks of life from the super wealthy in suites to the down and out who constitute a mini-community of rooftop dwellers, and hangers-out at a gay bar in the basement of the building. The book gives us a wealth of information about life in Cairo. We learn of the many foreigners who live in the city, many Christians, and how almost everyone manages to evade traditional Muslim norms. People drink and smoke.</span>
  <span id="freeText12577118735065959178" style="display:none">A soap opera story of the people who live in a Cairo mid-rise apartment building. We have all walks of life from the super wealthy in suites to the down and out who constitute a mini-community of rooftop dwellers, and hangers-out at a gay bar in the basement of the building. The book gives us a wealth of information about life in Cairo. We learn of the many foreigners who live in the city, many Christians, and how almost everyone manages to evade traditional Muslim norms. People drink and smoke. Young women learn to compromise their strict values to “get ahead” working for the dirty old men who run businesses and shops. Even gays manage to have their own special spots and night clubs. The plot follows the lives of a few folks in detail. A wealthy homosexual; a rich businessman who takes a second wife late in life and earns his sister’s undying hate, and a few street-smart con men. A good part of the story follows one young couple: he is religious and an idealist and wants to be a police officer; she is street-smart and is one of those willing to compromise herself to get ahead. He ends up falsely accused of ant-government activity and is imprisoned and tortured. In the ends turns to anti-government terrorism. A good book with a lot of local color of Cairo in the 1990’s. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12577118735065959178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_178312243" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178312243" />
    <a title="David Lentz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz"><img alt="David Lentz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473090881p2/3604115.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178312243?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Lentz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Lentz" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz">David Lentz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178312243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14159234534940607307">I read this novel, received as a Christmas gift, shortly after returning from a business trip in the Mideast. I was moved by its poignant sense of perspective, the elegant beauty of both the writing and the translation and the heart-wrenching grief that I found within its modest number of pages. The words leapt off every page as the tragedies emerged of characters whose chief links among each other had to do with their various and sundry connections to their dwelling place, an old building in Ca</span>
  <span id="freeText14159234534940607307" style="display:none">I read this novel, received as a Christmas gift, shortly after returning from a business trip in the Mideast. I was moved by its poignant sense of perspective, the elegant beauty of both the writing and the translation and the heart-wrenching grief that I found within its modest number of pages. The words leapt off every page as the tragedies emerged of characters whose chief links among each other had to do with their various and sundry connections to their dwelling place, an old building in Cairo. The stories were truly moving and every character was beautifully rendered and Al Aswany breathed life into them all with remarkable nuance, credibility, insight and empathy. Although his characters experience rough treatment at the hands of life in Egypt, the narrative remains tender and treats the reader as if he were a guest in the novelist's home. The language is simple but elgantly crafted and I don't know whether to give credit to author and/or translator but the team presented a beautifully published hardcover from the American University in Cairo Press. It was a joy to read this touching, sincere and genuine window into life in Cairo, such an articulate perspective of which is all too rare for those seeking to understand life amid the ancient turmoil of this often bewildering region. I was intrigued to find just how bewildering it is even to those who live there as well as to those who seek to understand it from a great distance. If anything will shed light across cultures so at variance, it is likely to be under the tent of art and music and creative literature that we may rightly hope and aspire to share a common oasis.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14159234534940607307" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9772083949" href="/book/show/2347744._">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1010975-a?shelf=%D8%B1%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%8A%D8%A7%D8%AA-%D9%88%D8%A7%D9%82%D8%B9%D9%8A%D8%A9">روايات-واقعية</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24867126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11244373271195472203"><br/><br/><br/>The writer has the ability to steal the reader and race him with his lines. He keeps firm grip on the reader's mind...even when you leave the book you keep thinking about the events and when will you go back to read more. You can live easily inside his story lines.<br/><br/>I have not seen the movie but I've seen some of the tv episodes, and i think the book is much much more exciting.<br/><br/>I'm thinking of buying the English version to compare it with the original Arabic versions...also I look forward to readin</span>
  <span id="freeText11244373271195472203" style="display:none"><br /><br /><br />The writer has the ability to steal the reader and race him with his lines. He keeps firm grip on the reader's mind...even when you leave the book you keep thinking about the events and when will you go back to read more. You can live easily inside his story lines.<br /><br />I have not seen the movie but I've seen some of the tv episodes, and i think the book is much much more exciting.<br /><br />I'm thinking of buying the English version to compare it with the original Arabic versions...also I look forward to reading his other books.<br />Tuesday, June 24, 2008 <br /><br /><br /><br />Today, I reached page 125<br /><br />I like how he dives deep inside each of his characters and serve the reader the core of their mind and feelings and you feel as if you know them personally.<br /><br />I don't see any unnecessary details and nothing is kept mysterious so far...and for a moment I was wondering if the writer knows all those characters in real life. His characters seem so real and alive!<br /><br />What I did not like so far is the typos and the lack of correct punctuations. I'm reading the fifth edition and hoping that the next edition will have better presentation.<br /><br />Friday, June 27, 2008<br /><br /><br />My final notes-<br /><br />Few minutes ago I finished reading this novel. For a moment I thought I was watching an action movie that was filled with suspense.<br /><br />In this novel, the writer blends the stories of about five main characters in a way that keeps you as a reader sitting at the edge of your chair wanting to know what happens next.<br /><br /> He sets the scenes, describes his characters and gives them a voice, but you never feel the presence of the writer in any of his lines. He presents their thoughts as they believe in it, and he exposes their deep feelings as they sense it. He never criticizes or takes sides as if he is just a narrator.<br /><br />Alaa Elaswani, is like a talented carpet maker who knows where to put each thread and ends up with a piece of art which you may or may not like. He divided this novel into scenes that was well crafted. He knew how to start each scene and how to end it as if he’s writing scenarios for a play or a movie.<br /><br />With this novel, Alaa Elaswani, stepped into a new level in Arabic literature for the amount of realism and drama he used in constructing this novel. He was very successful in balancing many factors by giving equal amount of attention for each character and avoiding unnecessary descriptions. He reflected well the time and the political events that painted the background for each scene as their characters see it. <br /><br />Despite the unexpected dramatic ending of each story, you as a reader will experience some relief from the tension that has been building throughout the whole novel.<br /><br />I would like to see Alaa El-Aswani writing a mystery or a fictional horrifying novel!….Also I think he’d do great in history and adventures. <br /><br />Can’t wait to read his other books!<br /><br />Monday, June 30, 2008</span>

<div id="review_129689826" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129689826" />
    <a title="Anastasia Hobbet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2671549-anastasia-hobbet"><img alt="Anastasia Hobbet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442459034p2/2671549.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/129689826?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anastasia Hobbet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anastasia Hobbet" href="/user/show/2671549-anastasia-hobbet">Anastasia Hobbet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2671549-anastasia-hobbet?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer129689826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10171041019536687252">I'd heard so much about this novel that I expected more--not more in terms of story, because there's lots of meaty story in this book--but more in terms of imagery, lyricism, and in-depth character portrayal. The single character who truly stole my heart is the old swain Zaki Bey al-Dessouki, who reminds me warmly of the endearing and penniless old Prince Yakimov in Olivia Manning's Balkan Trilogy. An Egyptian acquaintance who reads a lot of literature in English and Arabic told me that Egyptian</span>
  <span id="freeText10171041019536687252" style="display:none">I'd heard so much about this novel that I expected more--not more in terms of story, because there's lots of meaty story in this book--but more in terms of imagery, lyricism, and in-depth character portrayal. The single character who truly stole my heart is the old swain Zaki Bey al-Dessouki, who reminds me warmly of the endearing and penniless old Prince Yakimov in Olivia Manning's Balkan Trilogy. An Egyptian acquaintance who reads a lot of literature in English and Arabic told me that Egyptians are a bit amazed by Al Aswany's success in translation in the West, because at home he's considered a bit of a joke, a sort of Joan Collins of Egypt. More on that in a minute. There definitely is some irritating superficiality in the writing, an unevenness of sincerity that pops up here and there. For instance, Al Aswany writes about an Islamist demonstration broken up by the police. One of his main characters is in the demonstration, supposedly taking full part in it, but Al Aswany never mentions him until the end of the scene, where the character's actions are sketched in brief, as if Al Aswany just remembered him. The women of the book are all the product of two molds: they're either young and gorgeous, like Busayna, or old and screeching, like Zaki's mad sister, Dawlat. Nevertheless, the book succeeds because it paints a compelling portrait of a real place in a real time, portraying ordinary and not so ordinary people in a country most Western readers know nothing about, or so little that it might as well be myth. If an English-language writer turned out a book like Al Aswany's about an apartment building in New York or London or Paris, native readers of English might turn up their noses at it. We know this territory too well, and we're tired of it. But everything Al Aswany's book tells us is news. He's not a great writer, but he's a fine story-teller, and he'll make you listen.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10171041019536687252" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2981126864" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2981126864" />
    <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour"><img alt="J.M. Hushour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442964894p2/17463213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2981126864?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.M. Hushour" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour">J.M. Hushour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2981126864" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17788440352524870281">Having read quite a bit of Middle Eastern literature over the years (including this one long ago), I'm always wary of and struck by the ubiquitous themes that dominate the genre. If they aren't harping on European colonialism, then they're complaining about the Israelis! That's why it is always refreshing to find a novel that is just simply about people and, sure, yeah, those perennial themes are always lurking there somewhere in the background, but what moves me most in novels is the ability fo</span>
  <span id="freeText17788440352524870281" style="display:none">Having read quite a bit of Middle Eastern literature over the years (including this one long ago), I'm always wary of and struck by the ubiquitous themes that dominate the genre. If they aren't harping on European colonialism, then they're complaining about the Israelis! That's why it is always refreshing to find a novel that is just simply about people and, sure, yeah, those perennial themes are always lurking there somewhere in the background, but what moves me most in novels is the ability for the dominant themes and ideas to be transplantable to anywhere at anytime. "Building" accomplishes this to a high degree, giving us the interlinked stories of a small band of characters from various parts of Egyptian society, all connected by the titular structure. There's the aging businessman and his overly domineering sister, the gay journalist and his blue-collar lover, the frustrated young guy who can't become a cop, his frustrated girlfriend who has to jerk off her boss in the back storeroom, the aspiring politician and his secret, second wife, and so on. <br />There's a lot here to shock and to furrow one's brow, but there are equal parts beauty and humor, even when things get downright dark and weird. This would be a good place to start for anyone interested in ME fiction!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17788440352524870281" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer617054345052627499"><br/> <br/> Al Aswany uses a building as the organizing principle of this well populated book. Each character inhabits a different part of the building and lives a different thread of the story. The pacing takes some delightful cues from that of Egyptian soap operas with a small cliffhanger at the end of each part. This does not become disjointed because the stories are woven from good strong skeins, twisty and brightly dyed. Some get snapped.<br/>Like all the best Egyptian stories, this one ends with a weddin</span>
  <span id="freeText617054345052627499" style="display:none"><br /> <br /> Al Aswany uses a building as the organizing principle of this well populated book. Each character inhabits a different part of the building and lives a different thread of the story. The pacing takes some delightful cues from that of Egyptian soap operas with a small cliffhanger at the end of each part. This does not become disjointed because the stories are woven from good strong skeins, twisty and brightly dyed. Some get snapped.<br />Like all the best Egyptian stories, this one ends with a wedding, which in Egypt end with dancing; that unbelievable dancing that subdues sorrow until joy comes in the morning.</span>

<div id="review_228111624" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228111624" />
    <a title="Ismail Elshareef" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5563642-ismail-elshareef"><img alt="Ismail Elshareef" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518496834p2/5563642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228111624?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ismail Elshareef" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ismail Elshareef" href="/user/show/5563642-ismail-elshareef">Ismail Elshareef</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5563642-ismail-elshareef?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228111624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1871388491503605516">This is Egypt. Its past of glorified and often lamented early 20th-Century grandeur as well as its present of turbulence and increasing desperation and repressiveness are artfully laid out for the reader to analyze, contemplate and invariably pity in this impressive literary work. This is Egypt that everyone knows well but rather not talk about. <br/><br/>The book captures the collective moral and physical destitution of the post-1970s Egyptian society like no other book has; at least none that I have eve</span>
  <span id="freeText1871388491503605516" style="display:none">This is Egypt. Its past of glorified and often lamented early 20th-Century grandeur as well as its present of turbulence and increasing desperation and repressiveness are artfully laid out for the reader to analyze, contemplate and invariably pity in this impressive literary work. This is Egypt that everyone knows well but rather not talk about. <br /><br />The book captures the collective moral and physical destitution of the post-1970s Egyptian society like no other book has; at least none that I have ever read. The countrywide air of angst and despair is so thick you could cut it with a knife. The characters are drawn so skillfully and so realistically their pain is so intense and palpable it makes you wonder why it took so long for a revolution to erupt. <br /><br />Speaking of characters, this book is rife with a diverse set of characters that are rich with complexity and alive with nuance. The author did an extraordinary job in taking the reader on an unforgettable journey through both the most inexplicable and most banal in Egyptian mores. <br /><br />Each character represents a distinct reflection of Egypt herself. Her defiance, innocence, bitterness, lightheartedness, fanaticism, tolerance, softness, austerity, corruption, venality, hypocrisy, cowardice and hope. <br /><br />Allegory definitely weighs heavily in this work of fiction. <br /><br />Zaki Bey el Dessouki is an aristocrat and a francophile now living a shadow of his pre-1952-revolution posh life. He drowns his disillusion with reality in the pleasures of the flesh. <br /><br />Zaki is a living reminder of an era that was once promising and hopeful, until Nasser came. Zaki recounts, "Abd el Nasser was the worst ruler in the whole history of Egypt. He ruined the country and brought us defeat and poverty. The damage he did to the Egyptian character will take years to repair. Abd el Nasser taught the Egyptians to be cowards, opportunists, and hypocrites." <br /><br />Zaki embodies Egypt's murdered past of carefree and happy days. "Cairo was like Europe." He laments thinking of that time. "It was clean and smart and the people were well mannered and respectable ... What times! Every night was filled with laughter and parties and drinking and singing. There were lots of foreigners in Cairo. Most of the people living downtown were foreigners, until Abd el Nasser threw them out in 1956." <br /><br />His sister, Dawlat, is the complete opposite. Her caustic and vitriolic bouts or rage defined her character since her children abandoned her and emigrated. Her goal in life was to make her brother's life a living hell, and she succeeds in doing so. <br /><br />Dawlat represents a generation angry at the circumstances that led to the demise of their social standing; a generation so bitter and unhinged at what happened to them they either leave and forget about the old country or stay and unleash their wrath on everyone around them, including their loved ones. <br /><br />Then there is Taha and Busayna. Two kindred spirits torn apart by reality. These pivotal characters tell the core story of Egypt. Their pure love tainted by poverty and their dreams drowned by a culture defiled by venality, nepotism, sexism, and classism. That is, in a nutshell, the story of Egypt. <br /><br />And then of course, there is Hagg Azzam, a pious Muslim and a rich man who decides to join the Egyptian Parliament. The Arab world is rife with the likes of Hagg Azzam. They are the kings of hypocrites, the heads of opportunists and the leaders of cowards. They are the embodiment of the culture that's held Egypt and the Arab world hostage to a faulty religion. <br /><br />Oppressing people and robbing them and killing them, all the while praising God in a sickening expression of sinister piety. <br /><br />In an exchange between Hagg Azzam and an influential member of Mubarak's National Democratic Party, the man says, "No Egyptian can go against his government. Some people are excitable and rebellious by nature, but the Egyptian keeps his head down his whole life long so he can eat. It says so in the history books. The Egyptians are the easiest people in the world to rule. The moment you take power, they submit to you and grovel to you and you can do what you want with them. Any party in Egypt, when it makes elections is in power, is bound to win, because the Egyptian is bound to support the government. It's the way God made them." <br /><br />Sadly, that was not a disillusioned perception of reality by the corrupt government. People did grovel. People did obey. The Egyptian psyche has been conditioned since Nasser took office to be subservient. <br /><br />This comes through in a correspondence between Taha and The President where Taha writes, imploring Mubarak to look into an injustice that has just befallen him. He goes on to say, "Your Excellency Mr. Presiden will see that your son ..." The submissive tone starts with the word "son." <br /><br />The use of "son" and "daughter" inherently suggests the fallibility and inferiority of the citizenry, like a child that's eager to please his parent. The government, in turn, uses the words "father of the people" to refer to the relationship between Mubarak and the Egyptian people. This sort of condescending rhetoric was used excessively by Mubarak prior to his ouster on February 11, 2011. <br /><br />Perhaps the most complicated character is that of Hatim Rasheed's, the token homosexual in this cast of "normal" people. Hatim is a successful, smart and good-natured man that lives in a society that considers him a pervert because of his sexuality. Hatim's lover, a simple rural man named Abduh, is a closeted homosexual with a wife and kid and illiteracy to boot. Hatim's tumultuous personal life offers the reader a glimpse of life for the educated liberals in Egypt. <br /><br />I can go on and on about every character in this book. They are definitely very rich and very well fleshed out, but I'll leave some of that for you to explore on your own.<br /><br />Reading this novel in the post-January-25th-revolution world makes me wonder about the fate of these characters in this "new" Egypt. Many of the the grievances expressed repeatedly by the author were what erupted the revolution. <br /><br />Will the likes of Hagg Azzam and his cohorts finally get thrown in jail once and for all? Will Taha's dreams be allowed to flourish? Will Hatim's too? Will Zaki be able to feel proud again? <br /><br />These are some of the questions you will be asking yourself as you're reading this book. It's very topical and extremely thought-provoking. <br /><br />The only gripe I have with this book is the the translation. It's at times awkward and almost inaccurate. Some nuances are lost, as it is with most translated works of literature. Being fluent in Arabic makes it easy for me to spot those errors, but they're probably a nonissue to the non-Arabic reader. <br /><br />Get this book and learn about the old Egypt, and when you're done, make a prayer that the new Egypt isn't anything like this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1871388491503605516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2342741709" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2342741709" />
    <a title="Autumn Futch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44214844-autumn-futch"><img alt="Autumn Futch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519318579p2/44214844.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2342741709?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Autumn Futch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Autumn Futch" href="/user/show/44214844-autumn-futch">Autumn Futch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2342741709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12302379598315818550">I don't know how a book manages to cover so many triggering, inappropriate, and grotesque topics in less than 250 pages, but this one does it. Though difficult to read at times--cringing at the flippant coverage of problematic issues, stepping away for a few days after the truly triggering imagery--this book offers insight about the decade before the revolution, the societal standards that still plague Egypt, and the lives of those merely trying to scrape by, reconciling their sins and their dev</span>
  <span id="freeText12302379598315818550" style="display:none">I don't know how a book manages to cover so many triggering, inappropriate, and grotesque topics in less than 250 pages, but this one does it. Though difficult to read at times--cringing at the flippant coverage of problematic issues, stepping away for a few days after the truly triggering imagery--this book offers insight about the decade before the revolution, the societal standards that still plague Egypt, and the lives of those merely trying to scrape by, reconciling their sins and their devotion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12302379598315818550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3395754052" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3395754052" />
    <a title="Sotiris Karaiskos" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18940246-sotiris-karaiskos"><img alt="Sotiris Karaiskos" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407195235p2/18940246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3395754052?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sotiris Karaiskos" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sotiris Karaiskos" href="/user/show/18940246-sotiris-karaiskos">Sotiris Karaiskos</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10322690-the-yacoubian-building">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18940246-sotiris-karaiskos?shelf=african-literature">african-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3395754052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5269288295984192364">An excellent mapping of Egyptian society a few decades ago through some parallel stories centered on a building in Cairo. In this building, which resembles a metaphor of Egypt itself, people from different classes coexist, with different mentalities, religious beliefs and moral principles. All of them, each from his or her side, try to do the best they can to create their personal happiness, making great efforts and equally great sacrifices, with different levels of success or failure. But they </span>
  <span id="freeText5269288295984192364" style="display:none">An excellent mapping of Egyptian society a few decades ago through some parallel stories centered on a building in Cairo. In this building, which resembles a metaphor of Egypt itself, people from different classes coexist, with different mentalities, religious beliefs and moral principles. All of them, each from his or her side, try to do the best they can to create their personal happiness, making great efforts and equally great sacrifices, with different levels of success or failure. But they all have the same big problem, the same common enemy: the system that governs and controls their country. Whether they are the big ones who have the financial strength, or the little ones who are just trying to find a job or just live as they want, sooner or later they come face to face with this system, they get hurt and so they turn to extreme actions, the dream of escape or the silent acceptance of reality.<br /><br />All this is highlighted through the stories of these people that the author tenderly bends over them and shares with us their dreams and frustrations. I can understand the popularity of this book in Egypt, but the good news for other readers is that this book seems accessible to everyone, even to those who have no idea about the political history and situation of this country. So all of us, without getting too confused, have the opportunity to see some things about Egyptian society but also to be moved by reading some very humane stories and this is definitely an ideal combination.<br /><br />Μία εξαιρετική χαρτογράφηση της Αιγυπτιακής κοινωνίας μερικές δεκαετίες πριν μέσα από μερικές παράλληλες ιστορίες με επίκεντρο ένα κτίριο στο Κάιρο. Σε αυτό το κτήριο, που μοιάζει με μία μεταφορά της ίδιας της Αιγύπτου, συνυπάρχουν άνθρωποι από διάφορες τάξεις, με διαφορετικές νοοτροπίες, θρησκευτικές πεποιθήσεις και ηθικές αρχές. Όλοι τους, ο καθένας από τη μεριά του, προσπαθούν να κάνουν ότι καλύτερο μπορούν για τη δημιουργία της προσωπικής τους ευτυχίας, κάνοντας μεγάλες προσπάθειες και εξίσου μεγάλες θυσίες, με διαφορετικά επίπεδα επιτυχίας ή αποτυχίας. Όλοι τους, όμως, έχουν το ίδιο μεγάλο πρόβλημα, το ίδιο κοινό εχθρό: το σύστημα που κυβερνά και ελέγχει τη χώρα τους. Είτε είναι οι μεγάλοι που διαθέτουν οικονομική ισχύ, είτε οι μικρότεροι που απλά προσπαθούν να βρουν μία δουλειά ή απλά να ζήσουν όπως θέλουν αργά ή γρήγορα έρχονται αντιμέτωποι με αυτό το σύστημα, πληγώνονται και έτσι στρέφονται σε ακραίες πράξεις, στο όνειρό της φυγής ή στην σιωπηρή αποδοχή της πραγματικότητας.<br /><br /> Όλα αυτά αναδεικνύονται μέσα από τις ιστορίες αυτών των ανθρώπων που ο συγγραφέας σκύβει με στοργή από πάνω τους και μοιράζεται μαζί μας τα όνειρα και τις απογοητεύσεις τους. Μπορώ να καταλάβω τη δημοτικότητα αυτού του βιβλίου στην Αίγυπτο, το ευτύχημα για τους υπόλοιπους αναγνώστες, όμως, είναι ότι αυτό το βιβλίο μοιάζει προσιτό για όλους, ακόμα και για αυτούς που δεν έχουν ιδέα για την πολιτική ιστορία και την κατάσταση αυτής της χώρας. Έτσι όλοι μας, χωρίς να μπερδευόμαστε ιδιαίτερα, έχουμε τη δυνατότητα να δούμε κάποια πράγματα για την αιγυπτιακή κοινωνία αλλά και να συγκινηθούμε διαβάζοντας μερικές πολύ ανθρώπινες ιστορίες και αυτός είναι σίγουρα ένας ιδανικός συνδυασμός.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5269288295984192364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2652555651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Blaine DeSantis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2994598-blaine-desantis"><img alt="Blaine DeSantis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496452073p2/2994598.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2652555651?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blaine DeSantis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blaine DeSantis" href="/user/show/2994598-blaine-desantis">Blaine DeSantis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10322690-the-yacoubian-building">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2652555651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4837101866860847730">Really enjoyed this book which contains snapshots of some of the residents of the Yacoubian Building, and once we are introduced to them we follow them for a period of time. Sex is big in this book, but not graphic sex, but rather sex seems to be pervasive in the lives of many of the residents, so do not let that put you off on this book. We are introduced to a young man trying to become a police officer, Islamic extremists, politicians, businessmen and schemers. Also some very nice women who ma</span>
  <span id="freeText4837101866860847730" style="display:none">Really enjoyed this book which contains snapshots of some of the residents of the Yacoubian Building, and once we are introduced to them we follow them for a period of time. Sex is big in this book, but not graphic sex, but rather sex seems to be pervasive in the lives of many of the residents, so do not let that put you off on this book. We are introduced to a young man trying to become a police officer, Islamic extremists, politicians, businessmen and schemers. Also some very nice women who may be the most redeeming characters in the books. We also see life for those who live on the roof of the building in small little iron cubicles. Written by a native Egyptian, this novel certainly rings true as to these small slices of life and society in general in Egypt. And luckily for us, we can find all of that in the residents of The Yacoubian Building!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2362343933" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2362343933" />
    <a title="Lyndsey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19000574-lyndsey"><img alt="Lyndsey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595467075p2/19000574.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2362343933?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyndsey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyndsey" href="/user/show/19000574-lyndsey">Lyndsey</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2362343933" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14150511819473424602">Set in the early 2000s in Cairo, the novel tells the story of daily struggles, class differences, religious fanaticism and life under dictatorship in an Egypt that is fighting with the Americans in the first Iraq war.<br/><br/>There are many diverse characters. Their commonality is only that they have a tie to the Yacoubian building, where the wealthy live in grand old apartments and the poor live in a community of shacks on the roof. Their lives intersect in expected and unexpected twists and turns.<br/><br/>What </span>
  <span id="freeText14150511819473424602" style="display:none">Set in the early 2000s in Cairo, the novel tells the story of daily struggles, class differences, religious fanaticism and life under dictatorship in an Egypt that is fighting with the Americans in the first Iraq war.<br /><br />There are many diverse characters. Their commonality is only that they have a tie to the Yacoubian building, where the wealthy live in grand old apartments and the poor live in a community of shacks on the roof. Their lives intersect in expected and unexpected twists and turns.<br /><br />What surprised me was how sexual the novel is. I learned as much about everyone’s sex lives as I did about the details of major events in a the plot. Sexually explicit narratives weren’t reserved for those who were outlandish or somehow ‘devient’; all characters - the young couple, the married couple, the gay couple, the Casanova - were treated equally in the unveiling of their bedroom behavior. It seems the author used the characters’ sexualities, histories and proclivities to explain their personalities and the faction of society they represent. The second effect is that all became more human, more understandable by virtue of having been revealed at their most vulnerable moments.<br /><br />I found myself wishing I had seen Cairo, or at least seen a film set in Cairo. Many scenes were an impressionistic sketch of a neighborhood or a bygone way of life. I felt I couldn’t really visualize them.<br /><br />Finally, I found the novel surprisingly optimistic - with one exception, the good seem to win out - and yet this (sadly) isn’t consistent with the status of social unrest, radical Islam, women’s rights in Egypt and the Middle East today. But perhaps that’s the point: despite the wars and violence, there are small stories about love, happiness and kindness that persist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14150511819473424602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
